        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="95b637dc2cf644b6908748678cb43dc7">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '3bf413eb-055c-4e87-a286-7ce036f0e961'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d17071756" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>New-fashioned electrode hosts for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are elaborately engineered to involve multifunctional active components that can synergistically conquer the critical issues of severe volume deformation and sluggish reaction kinetics of electrodes toward immensely enhanced battery performance. Herein, it is first reported that single-phase CoPS, a new metal phosphosulfide for SIBs, in the form of quantum dots, is successfully introduced into a leaf-shaped conductive carbon nanosheet, which can be further in situ anchored on a 3D interconnected branch-like N-doped carbon nanofiber (N-CNF) to construct a hierarchical branch-leaf-shaped CoPS@C@N-CNF architecture. Both double carbon decorations and ultrafine crystal of the CoPS in-this exquisite architecture hold many significant superiorities, such as favorable train-relaxation, fast interfacial ion-migration, multi-directional migration pathways, and sufficiently exposed Na<sup>+</sup>-storage sites. In consequence, the CoPS@C@N-CNF affords remarkable long-cycle durability over 10&nbsp;000 cycles at 20.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and superior rate capability. Meanwhile, the CoPS@C@N-CNF-based sodium-ion full cell renders the potential proof-of-feasibility for practical applications in consideration of its high durability over a long-term cyclic lifespan with remarkable reversible capacity. Moreover, the phase transformation mechanism of the CoPS@C@N-CNF and fundamental springhead of the enhanced performance are disclosed by in situ X-ray diffraction, ex situ high-resolution TEM, and theoretical calculations.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202305190-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202305190-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The uninterrupted exhaustion of fossil fuels accompanied with severe environmental pollution is driving the rapid emergence of high-efficiency power systems and devices. Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), with analogous manufacturing technology and redox mechanism to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), have sufficient development potential for grid electricity storage as a new-fashioned technologic innovation device while offering remarkable merits with affluent natural resources, superior security features, and environmental friendliness.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0001_R_d17071639e555" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Eng. J.">1</a>-<a href="#adma202305190-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0004_R_d17071639e555" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Chem. Rev.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Merely employing the electrode hosts utilized for LIBs to the SIBs, nevertheless, are far away from success, since sodium-ion (Na<sup>+</sup>) with larger ionic-radius and heavier ionic-weight than lithium-ion induces several critical challenges, such as severe structural deformation and agglomeration, slow Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics, and limited electrochemical activity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0005_R_d17071639e564" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">5</a>, <a href="#adma202305190-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0006_R_d17071639e564" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Energy Storage Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, breaking through the restriction of overlarge Na<sup>+</sup> radius to further seek applicable electrode hosts that can readily store the large Na<sup>+</sup> is highly desirable but confronts an unprecedented challenge, particularly for the exploration of advanced anode materials with high capacity and excellent Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction ability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0007_R_d17071639e576" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Energy Storage Mater.">7</a>, <a href="#adma202305190-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0008_R_d17071639e576" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J. Mater. Chem. A">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Transition metal sulfides (TMSs) in SIB systems are fashionable as the highly competitive candidates that have been turned out to achieve remarkable sodium-storage capability stemming from its multiple electron transfer mechanisms.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0009_R_d17071639e583" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Small">9</a>, <a href="#adma202305190-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0010_R_d17071639e583" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Generally, the reaction between Na<sup>+</sup> and TMSs relates to the complete conversion of transition metal to its metallic, thus affording excellent theoretical capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0011_R_d17071639e590" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, TMSs also manifest better safety by refraining from the generation of sodium dendrite in the low potential region relative to the materials based on the insertion Na<sup>+</sup>-storage mechanism.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0012_R_d17071639e598" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Small">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Driven by conspicuous features, various unique TMS materials have been engineered and widely applied in SIB systems. For instance, Gao et&nbsp;al. constructed pea-like MoS<sub>2</sub>@NiS<sub>1.03</sub>-carbon hollow nanofibers via an electrospinning and thermal treatment strategy, which can afford good rate capacity and long-cycle performance due to fast ion-diffusion kinetics and shorten electron/ion diffusion path.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0013_R_d17071639e608" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Carbon Energy">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sun et&nbsp;al. designed a compact nanostructure with densified S-doped carbon skeleton wrapped hetero Ni-Ni<sub>3</sub>S<sub>2</sub> using a spatially confined “edge-to-edge” method, achieving high-efficiency Na<sup>+</sup>-storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0005_R_d17071639e619" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Zhu et&nbsp;al. produced monodispersed In<sub>6</sub>S<sub>7</sub>/N,S co-doped carbon hollow microspindles by morphology-preserved thermal sulfurization to achieve theoretical-value-close specific capacity and superior rate performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0014_R_d17071639e629" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Despite these breakthroughs, the severe volume variation and structure collapse upon cycling by reason of repetitive Na<sup>+</sup> de-intercalation remains an unsolvable problem for TMSs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0015_R_d17071639e637" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Sci. Bull.">15</a>-<a href="#adma202305190-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0017_R_d17071639e637" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, the intrinsic semiconductor characteristics of TMSs would readily give rise to large charge-transfer resistance with sluggish ion-transport kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0018_R_d17071639e642" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Mater. Sci. Technol.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, how to realize the regulation of TMSs in crystalline features and morphological structure to withstand large volume fluctuation for their applicability use in SIBs is very necessary.</p>
         
         <p>Ternary metal phosphosulfide (MPS<i><sub>x</sub></i>, <i>M</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;Fe, Mn, Ni, and Co, etc.) with a peculiar 2D layered structure has been employed as a new electrode host, which perfectly integrated the advantages of TMSs and metal phosphides (MPs),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0019_R_d17071639e655" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Mater. Chem. A">19</a>, <a href="#adma202305190-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0020_R_d17071639e655" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Small Methods">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> endowing better Na<sup>+</sup>-storage capability and more suitable potential than TMSs and MPs. Meanwhile, the regulated electronic feature of MPS<i><sub>x</sub></i> is capable of atomic-level engineering, which triggers prominent chemical diversity and enhanced intrinsic activities, thereby inducing swifter charge conduction than unitary or binary layered materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0021_R_d17071639e666" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Energy Storage Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Not only that the particular 2D lamella structure of MPS<i><sub>x</sub></i> is generally identified as a desired architecture for fast Na<sup>+</sup> de-/intercalating thanks to the adequately exposed surface area, adjustable morphologies, and scalable/shortened channels.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0022_R_d17071639e677" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Compos. Commun.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Until now, a few ternary MPS<i><sub>x</sub></i> hosts, such as FePS<sub>3</sub>/C,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0023_R_d17071639e687" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">23</a>, <a href="#adma202305190-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0024_R_d17071639e687" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> MnPS<sub>3</sub>/rGO,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0025_R_d17071639e695" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Alloys Compd.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> BiPS<sub>4</sub>-CNT<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0026_R_d17071639e702" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Energy Storage Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and Cu<sub>3</sub>PS<sub>4</sub>/G,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0027_R_d17071639e712" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Chem. Eng. J.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> have been springed up and manifested good electrochemical characteristics in SIBs systems. Despite these desirable attributions, some unavoidable bottlenecks derived from the agglomeration of individual layers of MPS<i><sub>x</sub></i> on account of the Van der Waals interaction between them result in the reduced active surface area and sluggish reaction kinetics,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0028_R_d17071639e721" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Chem. Eng. J.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> further restraining the application possibility of MPS<i><sub>x</sub></i> in SIBs systems.</p>
         
         <p>The effect of particle size is very vital for the enhancement of electrochemical performance for the MPS<i><sub>x</sub></i> anode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0029_R_d17071639e735" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Small">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Quantum dots (QDs) with a size less than 10&nbsp;nm have emerged as the new-fashioned nanomaterials and caused a wide range of interest in photocatalysis, energy storage, and sensing due to their tunable and size-dependent electronic features.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0030_R_d17071639e740" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Coord. Chem. Rev.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, the QDs could functionally couple on two or 3D materials based on their massive flexibility on surface engineering,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0031_R_d17071639e746" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Acc. Chem. Res.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which are significant in the energy storage fields because they can effectively shorten Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion distance, enhance electrical conductivity, improve charge-transfer resistance, as well as maintain structural stability during the expansion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0032_R_d17071639e753" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Metal–organic frameworks (MOFs)&nbsp;formed from well-organized metal centers and organic ligands are widely employed as a sacrificial template to construct transition metal complexes with novel nanostructure and nanoporous carbon confinement, which can not only simultaneously enhance the mechanical stability and electrochemistry stability, but also facilitate the infiltration of electrolyte ions into the electrode material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0033_R_d17071639e759" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Rare Met.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, the pyrolyzation reaction of MOFs with rich nitrogen (N) species can yield an N-doped carbon matrix to greatly enhance the ion-transport activity of porous carbon for electrochemical storage applications.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0034_R_d17071639e764" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Sci.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is therefore highly desirable, yet challenging, to skillfully design ultrafine MPS<i><sub>x</sub></i> QDs, and then introduce them into a MOFs-derived carbon-based matrix under simple synthesis conditions to maximize the multifarious functions of the two for mitigating the intrinsic drawbacks of individual MPS<i><sub>x</sub></i> anodes.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we reported the synthesis of a hierarchical branch-leaf-shaped CoPS@C@N-CNF architecture that is in the form of a leaf-shape metal–organic framework derived conductive carbon-coated CoPS QDs in situ growing on 3D branch-like N-doped carbon nanofibers (N-CNF) via electrospinning technique, followed by the carbonization and simultaneous phosphorus-vulcanization. The fabricated branch-leaf-shaped ternary composites could integrate the superiorities of each component including favorable train relaxation, shortened ion-diffusion distance, sufficient electron and electrolyte supply, superior interfacial electron-mobility, open interconnected channels for Na<sup>+</sup>, and adequately exposed active sites. More importantly, this is the first time that single-phase CoPS QDs coupled with conductive carbon were engineered exquisitely as an SIBs anode to thoroughly explore the electrochemical Na<sup>+</sup>-storage features. Stimulated by these advantages, the CoPS@C@N-CNF afford superior Na<sup>+</sup> storage properties with high capacity, durable cycling stability, and good rate feature.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202305190-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202305190-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The hierarchical branch-leaf-shaped CoPS@C@N-CNF architecture was configured involving three main construction stages. As described in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0001">1a</a>, vast amounts of PAN nanofibers with an average diameter of ≈100&nbsp;nm were facilely achieved by simple electrospinning, which exhibit a 3D uniformly crisscrossed network framework with large macrointer-fiber pores (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information) to further employ as the branch-shape precursor of hierarchical configuration. After solution-based MOF (ZIF-67) growth, a well-defined 2D ZIF-67 nanosheet array with a leaf-shaped microstructure can be tightly and uniformly anchored on the smooth PAN nanofibers skeleton (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0001">1b,c</a>) forming a branch-leaf architecture, similar to the coupling of a great many of leaves on branches in trees. Further observation in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0001">1d</a> manifests that leaf-shaped ZIF-67 nanosheet has a smooth surface and a very narrow sheet-thickness of less than 10&nbsp;nm. Subsequently, the pyrolysis reaction of branch-leaf PAN/ZIF-67 film can be occurred at high-temperature carbonization, during which PAN nanofibers were transformed to N-CNF matrix, while the metal ions were reduced by means of the reductive atmosphere stemmed from the decomposition of ZIF-67 to further form tiny cobalt QDs packaged in the vertically arrayed carbon nanosheets, signed as Co@C@N-CNF. Field emission scanning electron microscopy (FESEM) images of the Co@C@N-CNF in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information) present the well-distributed and cross-linked nanosheet arrays on the surface of the N-CNF skeleton. Meanwhile, the phase of the formed carbonization product can be confirmed by X-ray diffraction (XRD) spectrum in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information), which manifests a cubic Co crystalline phase with a Fm-3m (225) spacing group (JCPDS# 88–2325). After the following simultaneous phosphorus-vulcanization process, these nanosheet arrays still perfectly inherit the original leaf-shaped morphology with a relatively wrinkled surface (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0001">1e,f</a>), and meanwhile, they are still vertically preserved and uniformly anchored on the N-CNF skeleton (denoted as CoPS@C@N-CNF). Such a configuration can afford a hierarchical porous structure for superior mechanical robustness and a 3D long-range electron conduction for fast reaction kinetics. Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) image of the CoPS@C@N-CNF indicates an ultrathin solid nanosheet structure with ≈0.6&nbsp;µm in length and 0.4&nbsp;µm in width (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0001">1g</a>). As represented in a magnified TEM observation (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0001">1h</a>), many open mesopores can be clearly observed on the carbon nanosheet, thereby endowing the rich channels for Na<sup>+</sup>-transport and shortening ion-diffusion distance. The high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0001">1i</a> reveals that the “card house” structure formed by ZIF-67 derived carbon perfectly encapsulates CoPS QDs. Additionally, a clear fringe with a spacing of 0.164&nbsp;nm can be belonged to the (311) plane of the CoPS, as described in the inverted Fourier transformation pattern of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0001">1i</a>. Meanwhile, the average CoPS QDs size in CoPS@C@N-CNF was counted through a TEM observation in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information), showing an average particle size of ≈2.63&nbsp;nm. As seen in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0001">1j</a>, the energy-dispersive spectrometer elemental analysis for all five elements Co, P, S, C, and N also revealed the uniform growth of carbon-“leaves” coated CoPS QDs on N-CNF skeleton as we design. As a control, we have also constructed the CoS@C@N-CNF and CoP<sub>2</sub>@C@N-CNF at the same treatment temperature for further comparison. As observed by the FESEM images (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5,S6</a>, Supporting Information), both samples present similar branch-leaf morphological characteristics as the CoPS@C@N-CNF, and meanwhile, the slight wrinkle and aggregation of nanosheets can be found.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202305190-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/92ea6a15-1cf8-4224-ac0e-6aabce5d954b/adma202305190-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/92ea6a15-1cf8-4224-ac0e-6aabce5d954b/adma202305190-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/94332610-29cd-497a-bf21-1eba9fbb8286/adma202305190-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/92ea6a15-1cf8-4224-ac0e-6aabce5d954b/adma202305190-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The crystallographic characters and phase compositions of three resultant products were examined by XRD. As manifested in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0002">2a</a>, all the reflection signals stemmed from contrast samples CoS@C@N-CNF and CoP<sub>2</sub>@C@N-CNF can be readily authenticated to the hexagonal CoS with a P63/mmc (194) space group (JCPDS# 75–0605) and monoclinic CoP<sub>2</sub> with a P21/c (14) space group (JCPDS# 77–0263). Observation from the XRD pattern of the CoPS@C@N-CNF, the intense peaks appeared at ≈28.5<sup>o</sup>, 33.0<sup>o</sup>, 37.1<sup>o</sup>, 47.4<sup>o</sup>, and 56.2<sup>o</sup> were well-matched with the (111), (200), (210), (220), and (112), and (311) planes of the cubic CoPS, accorded with the standard card of JCPDS# 62–4614. Particularly, no XRD reflection signals assignable to any impurities can be noted in the above three XRD spectra, declaring their excellent phase purity. The strong peak emerging at ≈1346.6&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> in Raman spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0002">2b</a>) is deemed to be the D-band of characteristic defect-related carbon, whereas the other situated at 1568.9&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> belongs to the G-band of sp<sup>2</sup> hybridized graphitic carbon.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0035_R_d17071639e898" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Power Sources">35</a>, <a href="#adma202305190-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0036_R_d17071639e898" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - ACS Nano">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the ratio of peak intensity for D- and G-band (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>), generally employed to elucidate the disorder feature of carbon materials, is concentrated in a range of 1.1 to 1.2 in three samples, signifying the pyrolytic carbon originated from ZIF-67 and PAN mainly exists as the predominantly amorphous carbon with the abundant defects,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0037_R_d17071639e910" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Sci.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which is favor for increasing reaction active-sites and optimizing conductivity nature of the samples. More interestingly, the <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> value of the CoPS@C@N-CNF is larger than that of the CoS@C@N-CNF and CoP<sub>2</sub>@C@N-CNF, which may be caused by the introduction of S and P elements in the carbon skeleton. Furthermore, the locally amplified Raman spectra of three samples in a range from 200 to 800&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> were performed (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). We found that a few obvious characteristic peaks located at ≈356. 415, 433, 613 and 671&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> were attributed to the assignable features of the CoP<sub>2</sub>, which is in good agreement with the previous report.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0038_R_d17071639e934" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The other two peaks located at ≈467 and 515&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> are provably made by <i>E</i><sub>g</sub> and <i>F</i><sub>2g</sub><sup>(2)</sup> models of the Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> related to surface oxidation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0039_R_d17071639e953" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J. Mater. Chem. A">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, a broad 2D band in three samples is visible at ≈2776&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>. The presence of this band is characteristic of the graphitic–carbon matrix and is in good agreement with the graphitic–carbon observed by TEM and HRTEM.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0040_R_d17071639e961" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Small">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To get specific information for the mass fraction of carbon in three samples, thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) tests are performed from 30 to 800&nbsp;°C in air. Observation from the TGA curve of the CoPS@C@N-CNF (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0002">2c</a>), there is a slight mass fading of 3.5&nbsp;wt.% with the increasing temperature from 30 to 200&nbsp;°C, assigning to the volatilization of adsorbed water and gaseous components on the sample surface. Subsequently, a rapid weight decline of 32.4&nbsp;wt.% occurred in a temperature range of 300 to 800&nbsp;°C may be caused by the oxidation and consumption of carbon to produce CO<sub>2</sub>, accompanied by the decomposition of the CoPS to Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> solid, and SO<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0041_R_d17071639e982" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Electrochim. Acta">41</a>, <a href="#adma202305190-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0042_R_d17071639e982" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Chem. Eng. J.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which finally is stable at ≈62.2%. According to the TGA results, the CoPS content in the CoPS@C@N-CNF composites is calculated as ≈54.5&nbsp;wt.%. Furthermore, the surface areas and pore-size distributions of three samples were performed using N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption experiments. As manifested in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8a</a> (Supporting Information), the N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherms of three samples exhibit the type IV character on account of the presence of mesoporous pores. Through calculation, the CoPS@C@N-CNF, CoS@C@N-CNF, and CoP<sub>2</sub>@C@N-CNF possess the specific surface areas of ≈13.8, 6.1, and 11.4&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and their average pore-sizes are ≈23.4, 25.9 and 33.1&nbsp;nm (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8b</a>, Supporting Information), respectively.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202305190-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0045f726-9d4b-4be0-9d41-7effb75df80d/adma202305190-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/0045f726-9d4b-4be0-9d41-7effb75df80d/adma202305190-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/dc086278-43f4-4ecd-bbbe-21c668a1c7d3/adma202305190-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0045f726-9d4b-4be0-9d41-7effb75df80d/adma202305190-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202305190-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202305190" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">XRD spectra (a), Raman spectra (b), and TGA profiles (c) of the CoPS@C@N-CNF, CoS@C@N-CNF, and CoP<sub>2</sub>@C@N-CNF. XPS spectra of the CoPS@C@N-CNF, CoS@C@N-CNF, and CoP<sub>2</sub>@C@N-CNF: d) XPS survey spectra; e) Co 2p spectra; f) S 2p spectra; g) P 2p spectra; h) C 1s spectra; i) N 1s spectra.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The surface chemical environments and electronic states of the three samples were ascertained by implementing X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) measurement. The comprehensive XPS spectra could be utilized to identify major characteristic signals linked to Co, P, S, C, O, and N elements (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0002">2d</a>). Of which the existence of O 1s peak in three XPS survey spectra is related to the slight surface oxidation of the samples in air. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0002">2e</a> displays the high-resolution XPS spectra of the three samples in the Co 2p region, which includes three spin-orbit characteristic peaks at the lower binding energies of 779.1, 781.3, and 785.8&nbsp;eV as well as three characteristic signals at the higher binding energies of 793.9, 797.4, and 802.7&nbsp;eV. In which two pairs of apparent spin-orbit splitting peaks at ≈779.1/793.9&nbsp;eV and 781.3/797.4&nbsp;eV belong to the 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and 2p<sub>1/2</sub> orbitals of Co<sup>2+</sup> as well as the 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and 2p<sub>1/2</sub> orbitals of Co─O band, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0008_R_d17071639e1048" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J. Mater. Chem. A">8</a>, <a href="#adma202305190-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0021_R_d17071639e1048" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Energy Storage Mater.">21</a>, <a href="#adma202305190-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0043_R_d17071639e1048" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - J. Mater. Chem. A">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, every spin-orbit involves two satellite signals at a higher binding energy, illustrating the existence of hybridization between Co<sup>2+</sup> levels and PS ligand orbitals.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0020_R_d17071639e1056" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Small Methods">20</a>, <a href="#adma202305190-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0044_R_d17071639e1056" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Il Nuovo Cimento D">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the high-resolution S 2p spectra of the CoPS@C@N-CNF and CoS@C@N-CNF in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0002">2f</a> present two spin-orbit doublets at the binding energies of 161.6 and 163.5&nbsp;eV associating with the S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and S 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, respectively, which are identified as the characteristic signal of Co─S bond.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0045_R_d17071639e1068" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A sulfur peak emerged at 164.8&nbsp;eV for the formation of the C─S bond,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0005_R_d17071639e1074" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> suggesting the successful introduction of S into the carbon matrix. Besides, the other one observed at 168.2&nbsp;eV matches well with the oxidized sulfur species of S 2p (C─SO<sub>x</sub>─C) on the sample surface, manifesting the presence of sulfates, since XPS analysis only reveals the surface chemical environment of the top few nanometers of a sample, thanks to inescapable partial surface oxidation of compositions upon exposure to the air.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0046_R_d17071639e1081" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">46</a>, <a href="#adma202305190-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0047_R_d17071639e1081" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Small">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As for the P 2p spectra of the CoPS@C@N-CNF and CoP<sub>2</sub>@C@N-CNF (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0002">2g</a>), the doublet peaks manifest the P 2p<sub>3/2</sub> orbital at 132.7&nbsp;eV and P 2p<sub>1/2</sub> orbital at 134.0&nbsp;eV attributing to P─O bond, while the position of the other two peaks at 128.8 and 129.8&nbsp;eV were regarded as the P 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and P 2p<sub>1/2</sub> orbitals of Co─P bond.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0021_R_d17071639e1101" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Energy Storage Mater.">21</a>, <a href="#adma202305190-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0048_R_d17071639e1101" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Adv. Sci.">48</a>, <a href="#adma202305190-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0049_R_d17071639e1101" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0002">2h</a>, the peak-deconvoluted C 1s spectra with four clear bond signals of C─C, C═C, C═N, and C═O are, respectively, situated to the binding energies of 284.2, 284.7, 285.8, and 287.2&nbsp;eV,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0050_R_d17071639e1109" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - J. Energy Chem.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the existing C═N bond further interprets that the N atom can be readily introduced into carbon lattice through the pyrolysis reaction of PAN and MOFs. The nitrogen state is generally treated as a vital factor to influence the electrochemical features of carbonaceous materials, therefore, the primary modalities of N-doped carbon lattice involve pyridine-N, pyrrolic-N, and quaternary-N,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0051_R_d17071639e1115" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Sci. Bull.">51</a>, <a href="#adma202305190-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0052_R_d17071639e1115" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Small">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> reflected by the binding energies of 398.3, 399.6, and 400.5&nbsp;eV (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0002">2i</a>), respectively.</p>
         
         <p>The redox mechanism of the CoPS@C@N-CNF together with the reference samples, that is, the CoS@C@N-CNF (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9a</a>, Supporting Information) and CoP<sub>2</sub>@C@N-CNF (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9b</a>, Supporting Information) during the Na<sup>+</sup>-insertion were roundly estimated. As recorded in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0003">3a</a>, during the first sodiation process, a relatively weak reduction peak emerged at 0.94&nbsp;V due to the generation of Na<sub>x</sub>CoPS (CoPS&nbsp;+&nbsp;<i>x</i> Na<sup>+</sup>&nbsp;+&nbsp;<i>x</i> e<sup>−</sup>&nbsp;→&nbsp;Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>CoPS) accompanied by the formation of solid–electrolyte interphase film,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0021_R_d17071639e1158" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Energy Storage Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> while another strong one manifested at 0.56&nbsp;V maybe caused by the irreversible rupture of P─S bond yielding Na<sub>x</sub>CoS and P,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0023_R_d17071639e1165" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> followed by the conversion of Na<sub>x</sub>CoS to metallic Co and Na<sub>2</sub>S. In addition, a keen-edged peak near 0.03&nbsp;V is due to the alloying reaction of P and Na<sup>+</sup> to yield Na<sub>3</sub>P, as well as Na<sup>+</sup> insertion into the carbon skeleton.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0023_R_d17071639e1182" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">23</a>, <a href="#adma202305190-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0042_R_d17071639e1182" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Chem. Eng. J.">42</a>, <a href="#adma202305190-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0053_R_d17071639e1182" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - J. Mater. Chem. A">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During the reverse forward scan process, a bump situated at 0.52&nbsp;V involves the dealloying process of Na<sub>3</sub>P to P (Na<sub>3</sub>P&nbsp;↔&nbsp;P&nbsp;+&nbsp;3 Na<sup>+</sup>&nbsp;+&nbsp;3 e<sup>−</sup>), and the other two obvious peaks at 1.36 and 2.01&nbsp;V are possibly indicative of conversion reaction from Co and Na<sub>2</sub>S to Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>CoS, as well as desodiation of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>CoS to CoS (Co&nbsp;+&nbsp;Na<sub>2</sub>S&nbsp;↔&nbsp;CoS&nbsp;+&nbsp;2 Na<sup>+</sup>&nbsp;+&nbsp;2 e<sup>−</sup>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0054_R_d17071639e1211" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Adv. Mater.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Next, P and CoS could afford capacity in subsequent cycles. These results are analogical to previously reported CoPSe anodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0021_R_d17071639e1216" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Energy Storage Mater.">21</a>, <a href="#adma202305190-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0054_R_d17071639e1216" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Adv. Mater.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Starting on the second loop, the well-overlapped CV curves illustrate the remarkable reversibility on the sodiation–desodiation processes. Meanwhile, the peak signals at 1.84 and 1.09&nbsp;V in the cathodic scan are, respectively, related to the sodium insertion to yield Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>CoS, and further conversion reaction of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>CoS to Co and Na<sub>2</sub>S. Additionally, another shoulder peak of ≈0.63&nbsp;V can be induced by the alloying process of Na<sub>3</sub>P. Next, ex situ XPS analysis at different initial desodiation/sodiation states was operated to further give clearer information about the change of valence state of the CoPS@C@N-CNF upon initial cycling. As ex situ XPS results described in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information), the survey spectra of the CoPS@C@N-CNF electrode at different states illustrate that the main elements include Co, P, S, C, N, Na, and F. As revealed in the high-resolution Co 2p spectra of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0003">3b</a>, as the electrode was discharged from 1.0&nbsp;V to a full state of 0.01&nbsp;V, the Co<sup>2+</sup> 2p peak obviously moved to a lower binding energy, and meanwhile, the corresponding peak area gradually decreases during this period, indicating the partial oxidization of Co (II) to Co (III). More importantly, a distinct characteristic peak raised from Co<sup>0</sup> 2p<sub>3/2</sub> was observed.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0055_R_d17071639e1245" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Electrochim. Acta">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Subsequently, the peak of Co<sup>0</sup> species vanishes and the peak area of Co<sup>2+</sup> 2p recovers its original state at a fully charged state of 3.0&nbsp;V. The S 2p spectra at selected potential points are depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0003">3c</a>. The peak at ≈163.5&nbsp;eV is assigned to S<sub>2</sub><sup>2−</sup>, attesting to a good chemical adsorption ability, while the other two peaks at 169.0 and 170.3&nbsp;eV can be regarded as the polythionate and thiosulfate signals.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0011_R_d17071639e1261" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">11</a>, <a href="#adma202305190-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0056_R_d17071639e1261" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - J. Mater. Chem. A">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0003">3d</a> reveals the XPS spectra for following up the evolution of the P 2p signal during the initial sodiation–desodiation processes. With the electrode discharged from 1.0 to 0.01&nbsp;V, the P 2p peak associated with the Co─P bond at ≈129.5&nbsp;eV vanishes, which cannot go back to its original state at a fully charged state of 3.0&nbsp;V, illustrating an irreversible redox reaction. In addition, the P─S bonding species at ≈133.5&nbsp;eV can be observed, being a sign of a P<sub>x</sub>S<sub>y</sub> residue due to side reaction with the electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0057_R_d17071639e1274" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202305190-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/78848b5b-2074-494f-a352-d382c97b8be2/adma202305190-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/78848b5b-2074-494f-a352-d382c97b8be2/adma202305190-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5dc913e6-59ab-427e-90c1-05e9a43960de/adma202305190-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/78848b5b-2074-494f-a352-d382c97b8be2/adma202305190-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202305190-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202305190" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV profiles of the CoPS@C@N-CNF electrode at 0.5&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. High-resolution XPS spectra of the CoPS@C@N-CNF electrode at different initial discharged and charged states: b) Co 2p spectra; c) S 2p spectra; d) P 2p spectra. e,f) In situ XRD patterns of the CoPS@C@N-CNF electrode during the initial cycle. Ex situ HRTEM images of the CoPS@C@N-CNF electrode at different initial discharged and charged states: g) discharged state of 1.0&nbsp;V; h) discharged state of 0.01&nbsp;V; i) charged state of 1.4&nbsp;V; j) charged state of 3.0&nbsp;V.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To acquire a more in-depth and comprehensive understanding of the CoPS@C@N-CNF for initial voltage-dependent de-/sodiation mechanism, in situ electrochemical XRD, ex situ HRTEM, and SAED tests during the first cycle were performed. As manifested in in situ XRD patterns (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0003">3e,f</a>). Apparently, several reflections about BeO and Be in a 2<i>θ</i> range of ≈37to&nbsp;50<sup>o</sup> were detected. During the discharging from open-circuit voltage to ≈1.0&nbsp;V, the first-step intercalation reaction of the CoPS was initiated with the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions, resulting in the vanishing of diffraction peaks of the original CoPS phase. Meanwhile, four weak peaks of intermediate Na<sub>2</sub>CoS<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS # 79–2415) emerged at the positions of 16.0<sup>o</sup>, 26.1<sup>o</sup>, 33.0<sup>o</sup>, and 38.3<sup>o</sup>, and the matched crystal planes are (110), (121), (211) and (141), respectively. As the Na<sup>+</sup> ion persistently inserted into the electrode, the potential dropped to 0.01&nbsp;V, that is, the fully-sodiated state. Clearly observed that the phase stemmed from the first intercalation reaction disappears, accompanied by the emergence of the crystalline planes (400), (500), and (542) of Na<sub>2</sub>S (JCPDS # 47–0178) at ≈22.4<sup>o</sup>, 27.9<sup>o</sup>, and 37.7<sup>o</sup>, as well as (111) facets of metallic Co at ≈45.9<sup>o</sup> (JCPDS # 88–2325), forcefully affording the evidence for conversion reaction from Na<sub>2</sub>CoS<sub>2</sub> to Na<sub>2</sub>S and Co. Additionally, the appearance of Na<sub>3</sub>P peak signal (JCPDS # 74–1164) at ≈36.1<sup>o</sup> indicates the existence of the alloying reaction between P and Na<sup>+</sup>. After that, reversely increasing the potential to ≈1.4&nbsp;V, the expected reversal conversion and dealloying reaction occurred, as supported by the disappearance of Co, Na<sub>2</sub>S, and Na<sub>3</sub>P peaks together with the re-emergence of Na<sub>2</sub>CoS<sub>2</sub>. Further charging to 3.0&nbsp;V, however, the Na<sub>2</sub>CoS<sub>2</sub> peaks at 16.0<sup>o</sup>, 33.0<sup>o</sup>, and 38.3<sup>o</sup> were still detected, demonstrating the CoPS cannot be completely recovered its original crystalline phase during the first desodiation process, which is consistent with the reported CoPSe anode for SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0021_R_d17071639e1371" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Energy Storage Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> What is noteworthy that no characteristic reflection signals originated from phosphorus were detected, which may be thanks to its small crystallite size or amorphous feature during the whole first cycle. More significantly, the phase transformation of the CoPS@C@N-CNF during the first sodiation- desodiation loop can also be verified by ex situ HRTEM and SAED (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information) measurements. When the potential dropped to 1.0&nbsp;V, the intermediate product of Na<sub>2</sub>CoS<sub>2</sub> was fully produced as illustrated by the clearly detectable lattice fringes of 0.259 and 0.272&nbsp;nm in the HRTEM image of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0003">3g</a>, which were associated with its (112) and (211) crystallographic planes, respectively. Further decreasing the voltage to 0.01&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0003">3h</a>), the final discharge products with the d-spacings of 0.204, 0.215, and 0.342&nbsp;nm could be assigned to the (220) plane of metallic Co, the (110) plane of Na<sub>3</sub>P, together with the (323) plane of Na<sub>2</sub>S, respectively, revealing the occurrence of conversion and alloying reaction. As the electrode was continuously charged to 1.4&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0003">3i</a>), the lattice fringes of reversibly transformed phase (Na<sub>2</sub>CoS<sub>2</sub>) with (222), (202), and (112) facets emerged again because of Na<sup>+</sup> extraction, which can be maintained up to a charged state of 3.0&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0003">3j</a>), further suggesting the irreversibility of phase evolution. These observations are highly consistent with the in situ XRD results, further revealing that multistep conversion and alloying reactions happen between CoPS and Na<sup>+</sup> during an unremitting phase transformation.</p>
         
         <p>The electrochemical characters of the CoPS@C@N-CNF, CoS@C@N-CNF, and CoP<sub>2</sub>@C@N-CNF based on the cyclic lifespan, reversible capacity, and rate performance were systematically tested for their use as anodes in SIBs to comprehensively assess the significant superiority of metal phosphosulfide on sodium-storage ability. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0004">4a</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12a,b</a> (Supporting Information) present the galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) profiles of the three electrodes at 1.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, whose all-potential plateaus in their GCD profiles and the redox peak location of the corresponding CV curves manifest high consistency during the first cycling. Noteworthily, the CoPS@C@N-CNF electrode not only possesses more remarkable initial sodiation/desodiation capacities (663.9/549.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) as compared to that of reference samples, that is, CoS@C@N-CNF (507.8/391.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12a</a>, Supporting Information) and CoP<sub>2</sub>@C@N-CNF (381.7/214.2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12b</a>, Supporting Information), but also could afford an attractive initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) up to 82.9%, prominently exceeds those of two reference samples and previously reported MPS<i><sub>x</sub></i> and MPSe<i><sub>x</sub></i> anodes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0004">4b</a>). Moreover, the GCD curves of the CoPS@C@N-CNF electrode manifest gradual superposition with other profile outlines in the successive loops, uncovering the generally stabilized Na-storage feature. Subsequently, the cycling stabilities of the three electrodes were assessed at 1.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, as revealed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0004">4c</a>. Obviously, the CoPS@C@N-CNF electrode affords the highest reversible capacity reaching to 450.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 150 sodiation–desodiation loops, which is followed by the CoS@C@N-CNF electrode of 360.3&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>; whilst, the CoP<sub>2</sub>@C@N-CNF electrode achieves the lowest capacity of only 243.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Then, to further explore the morphology structure of the CoPS@C@N-CNF after cycling, we directly utilized the CoPS@C@N-CNF film as a binder-free SIBs anode for an electrochemical test at 1.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. After 50 sodiation–desodiation cycles, the film electrode is directly extracted from the coin cells to obtain more microstructural details. No serious structural collapse occurred and the 2D nanosheet structure can still be observed and tightly fixed on the N-CNF surface (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information), confirming a good structure durability. Following that, the comparison of rate capabilities for three electrodes was also assessed, as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0004">4d</a>. The CoPS@C@N-CNF electrode shows a good rate performance and gives the sodiation capacities of ≈504.4, 472.9, 430.2, 390.8, 440, 329.8, 262.9, and 205.2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with the rate changing from 0.2 to 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, 5.0, 10.0 and 20.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, while the sodiation capacities of the reference electrodes are unsatisfactory by applying same rate capability test, particularly for the CoP<sub>2</sub>@C@N-CNF electrode achieves a reversible capacity of only 61.6&nbsp;mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 20.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. In addition, a ≈100.0% of sodiation capacity (≈589.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) relative to the second cycled capacity can be recovered as the rate switched back to the initial 0.2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, illustrating good retention and excellent structural endurance of the CoPS@C@N-CNF. Through comparing the GCD curves of rate performance, the CoPS@C@N-CNF electrode also shows a smaller voltage polarization and lower overpotential than the other two (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information), demonstrating the significant reaction kinetics and stable Na<sup>+</sup>-diffusion ability. The plots of specific capacity versus rate for the most reported MPS<i><sub>x</sub></i> and MPSe<i><sub>x</sub></i> anodes in SIBs are exhibited by a diagrammatic representation in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0004">4e</a>. Remarkably, the rate capability of the CoPS@C@N-CNF stands out among those of SIBs anodes,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0019_R_d17071639e1503" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Mater. Chem. A">19</a>, <a href="#adma202305190-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0028_R_d17071639e1503" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Chem. Eng. J.">28</a>, <a href="#adma202305190-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0054_R_d17071639e1503" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Adv. Mater.">54</a>, <a href="#adma202305190-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0058_R_d17071639e1503" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Nano-Micro Lett.">58</a>-<a href="#adma202305190-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0062_R_d17071639e1503" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">62</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> particularly at a high rate over 5.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. These outstanding superiorities endow the great application possibilities of the CoPS@C@N-CNF in the advanced energy storage fields. The electrochemical impedance spectra of the three electrodes were conducted to further make a thorough inquiry for the significant properties of the CoPS@C@N-CNF (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0004">4f</a>). Nyquist profiles of these electrodes have a typical shape both referring to a semicircle of the high-frequency region assigning to the charge-transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) as well as a linear fragment of the low-frequency zone representing the Warburg diffusion process, respectively. More details about the equivalent circuit and fitting parameters obtained from the Nyquist plot are afforded in the inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0004">4f</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). It is found that the CoPS@C@N-CNF achieves a smaller <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of ≈126.5&nbsp;Ω than that of the CoS@C@N-CNF (264.3&nbsp;Ω) and the CoP<sub>2</sub>@C@N-CNF (287.6&nbsp;Ω), illustrating its fastest charge-transfer amongst these electrodes. Besides, according to the correlation between angular velocity and real part in the low-frequency zoon (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0004">4g</a>), the relatively lowest Warburg coefficient of the CoPS@C@N-CNF electrode calculated as 29.3 symbolizes its most outstanding Na<sup>+</sup>-migration kinetics in three electrodes, thereby endowing the most outstanding rate capability. These significant transportation features of ions and electrons are induced by the hierarchical branch-leaf-shaped CoPS QDs architecture with multi-dimensional ion-transport channels. Furthermore, a long-cycle electrochemical performance of the CoPS@C@N-CNF was tested under an ultra-high rate. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0004">4h</a> affords the sectional GCD curves of the CoPS@C@N-CNF electrode upon cycling at 20.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> that a complete sodiation–desodiation procedure takes only 60.3&nbsp;s to finish. Meanwhile, almost the same profiles uncover the stable phase evolution of the CoPS@C@N-CNF during the continuous long-loop testing. As manifested in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0004">4i</a>, the specific capacities of 287.7 and 126.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at a high rate of 20.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> can be achieved initially and over 10&nbsp;000 cycles, respectively, delivering an average CE as high as ≈100% and a capacity fading of only 0.016% per cycle. Finally, we summed up the latest reports related to MPS<i><sub>x</sub></i>- and MPSe<i><sub>x</sub></i>-based anodes for SIBs. Through comparison, the CoPS@C@N-CNF with multi-dimensional ion-transport channels possess more comprehensive and significant electrochemical Na<sup>+</sup>-storage ability, as exhibited in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202305190-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f8f1bbaa-a420-4553-8167-c521db07a50a/adma202305190-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/f8f1bbaa-a420-4553-8167-c521db07a50a/adma202305190-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e6afc7a0-fbb9-4e06-94bf-5218a7137180/adma202305190-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f8f1bbaa-a420-4553-8167-c521db07a50a/adma202305190-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202305190-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202305190" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Voltage–capacity curves of the CoPS@C@N-CNF electrode at 1.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Comparison of ICE b) and reversible capacity at different rates e) of the CoPS@C@N-CNF with the congeneric anodes previously published. Cycle performance for 150 cycles at 1.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (c) and rate capability (d) of the three electrodes. Nyquist plots with corresponding equivalent circuit (f) and the relationship of <i>ω</i>1/2 versus Z′ curves (g) of three electrodes. h) Partial GCD profiles of the CoPS@C@N-CNF electrode at 20.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. i) Long-term cycle test of the CoPS@C@N-CNF electrode at 20.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <div class="paragraph-element">To further clarify the discrepancy in rate capability of the three electrodes, the kinetics process in the electrode was analyzed in detail by distinguishing the contributions of the surface-capacitive and ion-diffusion behaviors with the aid of cyclic voltammetry (CV) at diverse scan rates, varied from 0.2 to 2.0&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. As presented in the CV curves of the three electrodes (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305190-fig-0005">5a</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18a,d</a>, Supporting Information), the CV curves of each electrode exhibit almost the same profile outline and trend at diverse scan rates, meanwhile the redox peaks were well conserved even reaching a high rate of 2.0&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>, revealing the rapid electrochemical process with no distinct phase transition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0027_R_d17071639e1605" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Chem. Eng. J.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, the position of reduction peaks slightly migrates to a more negative voltage, whereas the oxidation peaks shift just in the opposite direction with the increasing scan rate, standing for the polarization increasing. To judge whether the current is controlled by the surface-capacitive or ion-diffusion reaction can be given by employing the relationship between the peak current (<i>i</i>) and scan rate (<i>v</i>):<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305190-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0008_R_d17071639e1614" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J. Mater. Chem. A">8</a>, <a href="#adma202305190-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305190-bib-0017_R_d17071639e1614" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="adma202305190-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/c6f92b9d-eb57-4611-a16c-0737e790a3d8/adma202305190-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202305190:adma202305190-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/adma202305190-math-0001.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>i</mi>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                           <mi>a</mi>
                           <msup>
                              <mi>v</mi>
                              <mi>b</mi>
                           </msup>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}i = a{v}^b\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
            <div class="inline-equation" id="adma202305190-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/b2c0e725-3fd9-4886-8b85-97b98e7426ab/adma202305190-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202305190:adma202305190-math-0002" display="block" location="graphic/adma202305190-math-0002.png">

                        <mrow>
                           <mi>log</mi>
                           <mspace width="0.16em"></mspace>
                           <mi>i</mi>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">b</mi>
                           <mspace width="0.16em"></mspace>
                           <mi>log</mi>
                           <mspace width="0.16em"></mspace>
                           <mi>v</mi>
                           <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
